Title:  The Last of the Mohicans
Title:  
Title:  James Fenimore Cooper
Title:  
Title:  Author's Introduction
Title:  
Title:  It is believed that the scene of this tale, and most of the information
Title:  necessary to understand its allusions, are rendered sufficiently obvious to
Title:  the reader in the text itself, or in the accompanying notes. Still there is
Title:  so much obscurity in the Indian traditions, and so much confusion in the
Title:  Indian names, as to render some explanation useful.
Title:  
Title:  Few men exhibit greater diversity, or, if we may so express it, greater
Title:  antithesis of character, than the native warrior of North America. In war,
Title:  he is daring, boastful, cunning, ruthless, self-denying, and self-devoted;
Title:  in peace, just, generous, hospitable, revengeful, superstitious, modest, and
Title:  commonly chaste. These are qualities, it is true, which do not distinguish
Title:  all alike; but they are so far the predominating traits of these remarkable
Title:  people as to be characteristic.
Title:  
Title:  It is generally believed that the Aborigines of the American continent have
Title:  an Asiatic origin. There are many physical as well as moral facts which
Title:  corroborate this opinion, and some few that would seem to weigh against it.
Title:  
Title:  The color of the Indian, the writer believes, is peculiar to himself, and
Title:  while his cheek-bones have a very striking indication of a Tartar origin,
Title:  his eyes have not. Climate may have had great influence on the former, but
Title:  it is difficult to see how it can have produced the substantial difference
Title:  which exists in the latter. The imagery of the Indian, both in his poetry
Title:  and in his oratory, is oriental; chastened, and perhaps improved, by the
Title:  limited range of his practical knowledge. He draws his metaphors from the
Title:  clouds, the seasons, the birds, the beasts, and the vegetable world. In
Title:  this, perhaps, he does no more than any other energetic and imaginative race
Title:  would do, being compelled to set bounds to fancy by experience; but the
Title:  North American Indian clothes his ideas in a dress which is different from
Title:  that of the African, and is oriental in itself. His language has the
Title:  richness and sententious fullness of the Chinese. He will express a phrase
Title:  in a word, and he will qualify the meaning of an entire sentence by a
Title:  syllable; he will even convey different significations by the simplest
Title:  inflections of the voice.
Title:  
Title:  Philologists have said that there are but two or three languages, properly
Title:  speaking, among all the numerous tribes which formerly occupied the country
Title:  that now composes the United States. They ascribe the known difficulty one
Title:  people have to understand another to corruptions and dialects. The writer
Title:  remembers to have been present at an interview between two chiefs of the
Title:  Great Prairies west of the Mississippi, and when an interpreter was in
Title:  attendance who spoke both their languages. The warriors appeared to be on
Title:  the most friendly terms, and seemingly conversed much together; yet,
Title:  according to the account of the interpreter, each was absolutely ignorant of
Title:  what the other said. They were of hostile tribes, brought together by the
Title:  influence of the American government; and it is worthy of remark, that a
Title:  common policy led them both to adopt the same subject. They mutually
Title:  exhorted each other to be of use in the event of the chances of war throwing
Title:  either of the parties into the hands of his enemies. Whatever may be the
Title:  truth, as respects the root and the genius of the Indian tongues, it is
Title:  quite certain they are now so distinct in their words as to possess most of
Title:  the disadvantages of strange languages; hence much of the embarrassment that
Title:  has arisen in learning their histories, and most of the uncertainty which
Title:  exists in their traditions.
Title:  
Title:  Like nations of higher pretensions, the American Indian gives a very
Title:  different account of his own tribe or race from that which is given by other
Title:  people. He is much addicted to overestimating his own perfections, and to
Title:  undervaluing those of his rival or his enemy; a trait which may possibly be
Title:  thought corroborative of the Mosaic account of the creation.
Title:  
Title:  The whites have assisted greatly in rendering the traditions of the
Title:  Aborigines more obscure by their own manner of corrupting names. Thus, the
Title:  term used in the title of this book has undergone the changes of Mahicanni,
Title:  Mohicans, and Mohegans; the latter being the word commonly used by the
Title:  whites. When it is remembered that the Dutch (who first settled New York),
Title:  the English, and the French, all gave appellations to the tribes that dwelt
Title:  within the country which is the scene of this story, and that the Indians
Title:  not only gave different names to their enemies, but frequently to
Title:  themselves, the cause of the confusion will be understood.
Title:  
Title:  In these pages, Lenni-Lenape, Lenope, Delawares, Wapanachki, and Mohicans,
Title:  all mean the same people, or tribes of the same stock. The Mengwe, the
Title:  Maquas, the Mingoes, and the Iroquois, though not all strictly the same, are
Title:  identified frequently by the speakers, being politically confederated and
Title:  opposed to those just named. Mingo was a term of peculiar reproach, as were
Title:  Mengwe and Maqua in a less degree.
Title:  
Title:  The Mohicans were the possessors of the country first occupied by the
Title:  Europeans in this portion of the continent. They were, consequently, the
Title:  first dispossessed; and the seemingly inevitable fate of all these people,
Title:  who disappear before the advances, or it might be termed the inroads, of
Title:  civilization, as the verdure of their native forests falls before the
Title:  nipping frosts, is represented as having already befallen them. There is
Title:  sufficient historical truth in the picture to justify the use that has been
Title:  made of it.
Title:  
Title:  In point of fact, the country which is the scene of the following tale has
Title:  undergone as little change, since the historical events alluded to had
Title:  place, as almost any other district of equal extent within the whole limits
Title:  of the United States. There are fashionable and well-attended
Title:  watering-places at and near the spring where Hawkeye halted to drink, and
Title:  roads traverse the forests where he and his friends were compelled to
Title:  journey without even a path. Glen's has a large village; and while William
Title:  Henry, and even a fortress of later date, are only to be traced as ruins,
Title:  there is another village on the shores of the Horican. But, beyond this, the
Title:  enterprise and energy of a people who have done so much in other places have
Title:  done little here. The whole of that wilderness, in which the latter
Title:  incidents of the legend occurred, is nearly a wilderness still, though the
Title:  red man has entirely deserted this part of the state. Of all the tribes
Title:  named in these pages, there exist only a few half-civilized beings of the
Title:  Oneidas, on the reservations of their people in New York. The rest have
Title:  disappeared, either from the regions in which their fathers dwelt, or
Title:  altogether from the earth.
Title:  
Title:  There is one point on which we would wish to say a word before closing this
Title:  preface. Hawkeye calls the Lac du Saint Sacrement, the Horican. As we
Title:  believe this to be an appropriation of the name that has its origin with
Title:  ourselves, the time has arrived, perhaps, when the fact should be frankly
Title:  admitted. While writing this book, fully a quarter of a century since, it
Title:  occurred to us that the French name of this lake was too complicated, the
Title:  American too commonplace, and the Indian too unpronounceable, for either to
Title:  be used familiarly in a work of fiction. Looking over an ancient map, it was
Title:  ascertained that a tribe of Indians, called Les Horicans by the French,
Title:  existed in the neighborhood of this beautiful sheet of water. As every word
Title:  uttered by Natty Bumppo was not to be received as rigid truth, we took the
Title:  liberty of putting the Horican into his mouth, as the substitute for Lake
Title:  George. The name has appeared to find favor, and all things considered, it
Title:  may possibly be quite as well to let it stand, instead of going back to the
Title:  House of Hanover for the appellation of our finest sheet of water. We
Title:  relieve our conscience by the confession, at all events leaving it to
Title:  exercise its authority as it may see fit.
Title:  
Title:                 ----------------------------------------------
Title:  
Title:  Chapter 1
Title:  
Title:       Mine ear is open, and my heart prepared:
Title:       The worst is wordly loss thou canst unfold: -
Title:       Say, is my kingdom lost?
Title:  
Title:                                                             SHAKESPEARE
Title:  
Title:  It was a feature peculiar to the colonial wars of North America, that the
Title:  toils and dangers of the wilderness were to be encountered before the
Title:  adverse hosts could meet. A wide and apparently an impervious boundary of
Title:  forests severed the possessions of the hostile provinces of France and
Title:  England. The hardy colonist, and the trained European who fought at his
Title:  side, frequently expended months in struggling against the rapids of the
Title:  streams, or in effecting the rugged passes of the mountains, in quest of an
Title:  opportunity to exhibit their courage in a more martial conflict. But,
Title:  emulating the patience and self-denial of the practiced native warriors,
Title:  they learned to overcome every difficulty; and it would seem that, in time,
Title:  there was no recess of the woods so dark, nor any secret place so lovely,
Title:  that it might claim exemption from the inroads of those who had pledged
Title:  their blood to satiate their vengeance, or to uphold the cold and selfish
Title:  policy of the distant monarchs of Europe.
Title:  
Title:  Perhaps no district throughout the wide extent of the intermediate frontiers
Title:  can furnish a livelier picture of the cruelty and fierceness of the savage
Title:  warfare of those periods than the country which lies between the head waters
Title:  of the Hudson and the adjacent lakes.
Title:  
Title:  The facilities which nature had there offered to the march of the combatants
Title:  were too obvious to be neglected. The lengthened sheet of the Champlain
Title:  stretched from the frontiers of Canada, deep within the borders of the
Title:  neighboring province of New York, forming a natural passage across half the
Title:  distance that the French were compelled to master in order to strike their
Title:  enemies. Near its southern termination, it received the contributions of
Title:  another lake, whose waters were so limpid as to have been exclusively
Title:  selected by the Jesuit missionaries to perform the typical purification of
Title:  baptism, and to obtain for it the title of lake du Saint Sacrement. The less
Title:  zealous English thought they conferred a sufficient honor on its unsullied
Title:  fountains, when they bestowed the name of their reigning prince, the second
Title:  of the house of Hanover. The two united to rob the untutored possessors of
Title:  its wooded scenery of their native right to perpetuate its original
Title:  appellation of Horican.1
Title:  
Title:  Winding its way among countless islands, and imbedded in mountains, the holy
Title:  lake extended a dozen leagues still further to the south. With the high
Title:  plain that there interposed itself to the further passage of the water,
Title:  commenced a portage of as many miles, which conducted the adventurer to the
Title:  banks of the Hudson, at a point where, with the usual obstructions of the
Title:  rapids, or rifts, as they were then termed in the language of the country,
Title:  the river became navigable to the tide.
Title:  
Title:  While, in the pursuit of their daring plans of annoyance, the restless
Title:  enterprise of the French even attempted the distant and difficult gorges of
Title:  the Alleghany, it may easily be imagined that their proverbial acuteness
Title:  would not overlook the natural advantages of the district we have just
Title:  described. It became, emphatically, the bloody arena, in which most of the
Title:  battles for the mastery of the colonies were contested. Forts were erected
Title:  at the different points that commanded the facilities of the route, and were
Title:  taken and retaken, razed and rebuilt, as victory alighted on the hostile
Title:  banners. While the husbandman shrank back from the dangerous passes, within
Title:  the safer boundaries of the more ancient settlements, armies larger than
Title:  those that had often disposed of the scepters of the mother countries, were
Title:  seen to bury themselves in these forests, whence they rarely returned but in
Title:  skeleton bands, that were haggard with care or dejected by defeat. Though
Title:  the arts of peace were unknown to this fatal region, its forests were alive
Title:  with men; its shades and glens rang with the sounds of martial music, and
Title:  the echoes of its mountains threw back the laugh, or repeated the wanton
Title:  cry, of many a gallant and reckless youth, as he hurried by them, in the
Title:  noontide of his spirits, to slumber in a long night of forgetfulness.
Title:  
Title:  It was in this scene of strife and bloodshed that the incidents we shall
Title:  attempt to relate occurred, during the third year of the war which England
Title:  and France last waged for the possession of a country that neither was
Title:  destined to retain.
Title:  
Title:  The imbecility of her military leaders abroad, and the fatal want of energy
Title:  in her councils at home, had lowered the character of Great Britain from the
Title:  proud elevation on which it had been placed by the talents and enterprise of
Title:  her former warriors and statesmen. No longer dreaded by her enemies, her
Title:  servants were fast losing the confidence of self-respect. In this mortifying
Title:  abasement, the colonists, though innocent of her imbecility, and too humble
Title:  to be the agents of her blunders, were but the natural participators. They
Title:  had recently seen a chosen army from that country, which, reverencing as a
Title:  mother, they had blindly believed invincible - an army led by a chief who
Title:  had been selected from a crowd of trained warriors, for his rare military
Title:  endowments, disgracefully routed by a handful of French and Indians, and
Title:  only saved from annihilation by the coolness and spirit of a Virginian boy,
Title:  whose riper fame has since diffused itself, with the steady influence of
Title:  moral truth, to the uttermost confines of Christendom.2 A wide frontier had
Title:  been laid naked by this unexpected disaster, and more substantial evils were
Title:  preceded by a thousand fanciful and imaginary dangers. The alarmed colonists
Title:  believed that the yells of the savages mingled with every fitful gust of
Title:  wind that issued from the interminable forests of the west. The terrific
Title:  character of their merciless enemies increased immeasurably the natural
Title:  horrors of warfare. Numberless recent massacres were still vivid in their
Title:  recollections; nor was there any ear in the provinces so deaf as not to have
Title:  drunk in with avidity the narrative of some fearful tale of midnight murder,
Title:  in which the natives of the forests were the principal and barbarous actors.
Title:  As the credulous and excited traveler related the hazardous chances of the
Title:  wilderness, the blood of the timid curdled with terror, and mothers cast
Title:  anxious glances even at those children which slumbered within the security
Title:  of the largest towns. In short, the magnifying influence of fear began to
Title:  set at naught the calculations of reason, and to render those who should
Title:  have remembered their manhood, the slaves of the basest passions. Even the
Title:  most confident and the stoutest hearts began to think the issue of the
Title:  contest was becoming doubtful; and that abject class was hourly increasing
Title:  in numbers, who thought they foresaw all the possessions of the English
Title:  crown in America subdued by their Christian foes, or laid waste by the
Title:  inroads of their relentless allies.
Title:  
Title:  When, therefore, intelligence was received at the fort which covered the
Title:  southern termination of the portage between the Hudson and the lakes, that
Title:  Montcalm had been seen moving up the Champlain, with an army numerous as the
Title:  leaves on the trees, its truth was admitted with more of the craven
Title:  reluctance of fear than with the stern joy that a warrior should feel, in
Title:  finding an enemy within reach of his blow. The news had been brought, toward
Title:  the decline of a day in midsummer, by an Indian runner, who also bore an
Title:  urgent request from Munro, the commander of a work on the shore of the holy
Title:  lake, for a speedy and powerful reinforcement. It has already been mentioned
Title:  that the distance between these two posts was less than five leagues. The
Title:  rude path, which originally formed their line of communication, had been
Title:  widened for the passage of wagons; so that the distance which had been
Title:  traveled by the son of the forest in two hours, might easily be effected by
Title:  a detachment of troops, with their necessary baggage, between the rising and
Title:  setting of a summer sun. The loyal servants of the British crown had given
Title:  to one of these forest-fastnesses the name of William Henry, and to the
Title:  other that of Fort Edward, calling each after a favorite prince of the
Title:  reigning family. The veteran Scotchman just named held the first, with a
Title:  regiment of regulars and a few provincials; a force really by far too small
Title:  to make head against the formidable power that Montcalm was leading to the
Title:  foot of his earthen mounds. At the latter, however, lay General Webb, who
Title:  commanded the armies of the king in the northern provinces, with a body of
Title:  more than five thousand men. By uniting the several detachments of his
Title:  command, this officer might have arrayed nearly double that number of
Title:  combatants against the enterprising Frenchman, who had ventured so far from
Title:  his reinforcements, with an army but little superior in numbers.
Title:  
Title:  But under the influence of their degraded fortunes, both officers and men
Title:  appeared better disposed to await the approach of their formidable
Title:  antagonists, within their works, than to resist the progress of their march,
Title:  by emulating the successful example of the French at Fort du Quesne, and
Title:  striking a blow on their advance.
Title:  
Title:  After the first surprise of the intelligence had a little abated, a rumor
Title:  was spread through the entrenched camp, which stretched along the margin of
Title:  the Hudson, forming a chain of outworks to the body of the fort itself, that
Title:  a chosen detachment of fifteen hundred men was to depart, with the dawn, for
Title:  William Henry, the post at the northern extremity of the portage. That which
Title:  at first was only rumor, soon became certainty, as orders passed from the
Title:  quarters of the commander-in-chief to the several corps he had selected for
Title:  this service, to prepare for their speedy departure. All doubts as to the
Title:  intention of Webb now vanished, and an hour or two of hurried footsteps and
Title:  anxious faces succeeded. The novice in the military art flew from point to
Title:  point, retarding his own preparations by the excess of his violent and
Title:  somewhat distempered zeal; while the more practiced veteran made his
Title:  arrangements with a deliberation that scorned every appearance of haste;
Title:  though his sober lineaments and anxious eye sufficiently betrayed that he
Title:  had no very strong professional relish for the, as yet, untried and dreaded
Title:  warfare of the wilderness. At length the sun set in a flood of glory, behind
Title:  the distant western hills, and as darkness drew its veil around the secluded
Title:  spot the sounds of preparation diminished; the last light finally
Title:  disappeared from the log cabin of some officer; the trees cast their deeper
Title:  shadows over the mounds and the rippling stream, and a silence soon pervaded
Title:  the camp, as deep as that which reigned in the vast forest by which it was
Title:  environed.
Title:  
Title:  According to the orders of the preceding night, the heavy sleep of the army
Title:  was broken by the rolling of the warning drums, whose rattling echoes were
Title:  heard issuing, on the damp morning air, out of every vista of the woods,
Title:  just as day began to draw the shaggy outlines of some tall pines of the
Title:  vicinity, on the opening brightness of a soft and cloudless eastern sky. In
Title:  an instant the whole camp was in motion; the meanest soldier arousing from
Title:  his lair to witness the departure of his comrades, and to share in the
Title:  excitement and incidents of the hour. The simple array of the chosen band
Title:  was soon completed. While the regular and trained hirelings of the king
Title:  marched with haughtiness to the right of the line, the less pretending
Title:  colonists took their humbler position on its left, with a docility that long
Title:  practice had rendered easy. The scouts departed; strong guards preceded and
Title:  followed the lumbering vehicles that bore the baggage; and before the gray
Title:  light of the morning was mellowed by the rays of the sun, the main body of
Title:  the combatants wheeled into column, and left the encampment with a show of
Title:  high military bearing, that served to drown the slumbering apprehensions of
Title:  many a novice, who was now about to make his first essay in arms. While in
Title:  view of their admiring comrades, the same proud front and ordered array was
Title:  observed, until the notes of their fifes growing fainter in distance, the
Title:  forest at length appeared to swallow up the living mass which had slowly
Title:  entered its bosom.
Title:  
Title:  The deepest sounds of the retiring and invisible column had ceased to be
Title:  borne on the breeze to the listeners, and the latest straggler had already
Title:  disappeared in pursuit; but there still remained the signs of another
Title:  departure, before a log cabin of unusual size and accommodations, in front
Title:  of which those sentinels paced their rounds, who were known to guard the
Title:  person of the English general. At this spot were gathered some half dozen
Title:  horses, caparisoned in a manner which showed that two, at least, were
Title:  destined to bear the persons of females, of a rank that it was not usual to
Title:  meet so far in the wilds of the country. A third wore trappings and arms of
Title:  an officer of the staff; while the rest, from the plainness of the housings,
Title:  and the traveling mails with which they were encumbered, were evidently
Title:  fitted for the reception of as many menials, who were, seemingly, already
Title:  waiting the pleasure of those they served. At a respectful distance from
Title:  this unusual show, were gathered divers groups of curious idlers; some
Title:  admiring the blood and bone of the high-mettled military charger, and others
Title:  gazing at the preparations, with the dull wonder of vulgar curiosity. There
Title:  was one man, however, who, by his countenance and actions, formed a marked
Title:  exception to those who composed the latter class of spectators, being
Title:  neither idle, nor seemingly very ignorant.
Title:  
Title:  The person of this individual was to the last degree ungainly, without being
Title:  in any particular manner deformed. He had all the bones and joints of other
Title:  men, without any of their proportions. Erect, his stature surpassed that of
Title:  his fellows; though seated, he appeared reduced within the ordinary limits
Title:  of the race. The same contrariety in his members seemed to exist throughout
Title:  the whole man. His head was large; his shoulders narrow; his arms long and
Title:  dangling; while his hands were small, if not delicate. His legs and thighs
Title:  were thin, nearly to emaciation, but of extraordinary length; and his knees
Title:  would have been considered tremendous, had they not been outdone by the
Title:  broader foundations on which this false superstructure of blended human
Title:  orders was so profanely reared. The ill-assorted and injudicious attire of
Title:  the individual only served to render his awkwardness more conspicuous. A
Title:  sky-blue coat, with short and broad skirts and low cape, exposed a long,
Title:  thin neck, and longer and thinner legs, to the worst animadversions of the
Title:  evil-disposed. His nether garment was a yellow nankeen, closely fitted to
Title:  the shape, and tied at his bunches of knees by large knots of white ribbon,
Title:  a good deal sullied by use. Clouded cotton stockings, and shoes, on one of
Title:  the latter of which was a plated spur, completed the costume of the lower
Title:  extremity of this figure, no curve or angle of which was concealed, but, on
Title:  the other hand, studiously exhibited, through the vanity or simplicity of
Title:  its owner. From beneath the flap of an enormous pocket of a soiled vest of
Title:  embossed silk, heavily ornamented with tarnished silver lace, projected an
Title:  instrument, which, from being seen in such martial company, might have been
Title:  easily mistaken for some mischievous and unknown implement of war. Small as
Title:  it was, this uncommon engine had excited the curiousity of most of the
Title:  Europeans in the camp, though several of the provincials were seen to handle
Title:  it, not only without fear, but with the utmost familiarity. A large, civil
Title:  cocked hat, like those worn by clergymen within the last thirty years,
Title:  surmounted the whole, furnishing dignity to a good-natured and somewhat
Title:  vacant countenance, that apparently needed such artificial aid, to support
Title:  the gravity of some high and extraordinary trust.
Title:  
Title:  While the common herd stood aloof, in deference to the quarters of Webb, the
Title:  figure we have described stalked into the center of the domestics, freely
Title:  expressing his censures or commendations on the merits of the horses, as by
Title:  chance they displeased or satisfied his judgment.
Title:  
Title:  This beast, I rather conclude, friend, is not of home raising, but is from
Title:  foreign lands, or perhaps from the little island itself over the blue water?
Title:  he said, in a voice as remarkable for the softness and sweetness of its
Title:  tones, as was his person for its rare proportions; I may speak of these
Title:  things, and be no braggart; for I have been down at both havens; that which
Title:  is situate at the mouth of Thames, and is named after the capital of Old
Title:  England, and that which is called Haven, with the addition of the word New';
Title:  and have seen the scows and brigantines collecting their droves, like the
Title:  gathering to the ark, being outward bound to the Island of Jamaica, for the
Title:  purpose of barter and traffic in four-footed animals; but never before have
Title:  I beheld a beast which verified the true scripture war-horse like this: He
Title:  paweth in the valley, and rejoiceth in his strength; he goeth on to meet the
Title:  armed men. He saith among the trumpets, Ha, ha; and he smelleth the battle
Title:  afar off, the thunder of the captains, and the shouting. - It would seem
Title:  that the stock of the horse of Israel had descended to our own time; would
Title:  it not, friend?
Title:  
Title:  Receiving no reply to this extraordinary appeal, which in truth, as it was
Title:  delivered with the vigor of full and sonorous tones, merited some sort of
Title:  notice, he who had thus sung forth the language of the holy book turned to
Title:  the silent figure to whom he had unwittingly addressed himself, and found a
Title:  new and more powerful subject of admiration in the object that encountered
Title:  his gaze. His eyes fell on the still, upright, and rigid form of the Indian
Title:  runner, who had borne to the camp the unwelcome tidings of the preceding
Title:  evening. Although in a state of perfect repose, and apparently disregarding,
Title:  with characteristic stoicism, the excitement and bustle around him, there
Title:  was a sullen fierceness mingled with the quiet of the savage, that was
Title:  likely to arrest the attention of much more experienced eyes than those
Title:  which now scanned him, in unconcealed amazement. The native bore both the
Title:  tomahawk and knife of his tribe; and yet his appearance was not altogether
Title:  that of a warrior. On the contrary, there was an air of neglect about his
Title:  person, like that which might have proceeded from great and recent exertion,
Title:  which he had not yet found leisure to repair. The colors of the war-paint
Title:  had blended in dark confusion about his fierce countenance, and rendered his
Title:  swarthy lineaments still more savage and repulsive than if art had attempted
Title:  an effect which had been thus produced by chance. His eye, alone, which
Title:  glistened like a fiery star amid lowering clouds, was to be seen in its
Title:  state of native wildness. For a single instant his searching and yet wary
Title:  glance met the wondering look of the other, and then changing its direction,
Title:  partly in cunning, and partly in disdain, it remained fixed, as if
Title:  penetrating the distant air.
Title:  
Title:  It is impossible to say what unlooked-for remark this short and silent
Title:  communication, between two such singular men, might have elicited from the
Title:  white man, had not his active curiosity been again drawn to other objects. A
Title:  general movement among the domestics, and a low sound of gentle voices,
Title:  announced the approach of those whose presence alone was wanted to enable
Title:  the cavalcade to move. The simple admirer of the war-horse instantly fell
Title:  back to a low, gaunt, switch-tailed mare, that was unconsciously gleaning
Title:  the faded herbage of the camp nigh by; where, leaning with one elbow on the
Title:  blanket that concealed an apology for a saddle, he became a spectator of the
Title:  departure, while a foal was quietly making its morning repast, on the
Title:  opposite side of the same animal.
Title:  
Title:  A young man, in the dress of an officer, conducted to their steeds two
Title:  females, who, as it was apparent by their dresses, were prepared to
Title:  encounter the fatigues of a journey in the woods. One, and she was the more
Title:  juvenile in her appearance, though both were young, permitted glimpses of
Title:  her dazzling complexion, fair golden hair, and bright blue eyes, to be
Title:  caught, as she artlessly suffered the morning air to blow aside the green
Title:  veil which descended low from her beaver.
Title:  
Title:  The flush which still lingered above the pines in the western sky was not
Title:  more bright nor delicate than the bloom on her cheek; nor was the opening
Title:  day more cheering than the animated smile which she bestowed on the youth,
Title:  as he assisted her into the saddle. The other, who appeared to share equally
Title:  in the attention of the young officer, concealed her charms from the gaze of
Title:  the soldiery with a care that seemed better fitted to the experience of four
Title:  or five additional years. It could be seen, however, that her person, though
Title:  molded with the same exquisite proportions, of which none of the graces were
Title:  lost by the traveling dress she wore, was rather fuller and more mature than
Title:  that of her companion.
Title:  
Title:  No sooner were these females seated, than their attendant sprang lightly
Title:  into the saddle of the war-horse, when the whole three bowed to Webb, who in
Title:  courtesy, awaited their parting on the threshold of his cabin and turning
Title:  their horses' heads, they proceeded at a slow amble, followed by their
Title:  train, toward the northern entrance of the encampment. As they traversed
Title:  that short distance, not a voice was heard among them; but a slight
Title:  exclamation proceeded from the younger of the females, as the Indian runner
Title:  glided by her, unexpectedly, and led the way along the military road in her
Title:  front. Though this sudden and startling movement of the Indian produced no
Title:  sound from the other, in the surprise her veil also was allowed to open its
Title:  folds, and betrayed an indescribable look of pity, admiration, and horror,
Title:  as her dark eye followed the easy motions of the savage. The tresses of this
Title:  lady were shining and black, like the plumage of the raven. Her complexion
Title:  was not brown, but it rather appeared charged with the color of the rich
Title:  blood, that seemed ready to burst its bounds. And yet there was neither
Title:  coarseness nor want of shadowing in a countenance that was exquisitely
Title:  regular, and dignified and surpassingly beautiful. She smiled, as if in pity
Title:  at her own momentary forgetfulness, discovering by the act a row of teeth
Title:  that would have shamed the purest ivory; when, replacing the veil, she bowed
Title:  her face, and rode in silence, like one whose thoughts were abstracted from
Title:  the scene around her.
Title:  
Title:  -----------------------
Title:  
Title:  1 As each nation of the Indians had its language or its dialect, they
Title:  usually gave different names to the same places, though nearly all of their
Title:  appellations were descriptive of the object. Thus a literal translation of
Title:  the name of this beautiful sheet of water, used by the tribe that dwelt on
Title:  its banks, would be The Tail of the Lake. Lake George, as it is vulgarly,
Title:  and now, indeed, legally, called, forms a sort of tail to Lake Champlain,
Title:  when viewed on the map. Hence, the name.
Title:  
Title:  2 Washington, who, after uselessly admonishing the European general of the
Title:  danger into which he was heedlessly running, saved the remnants of the
Title:  British army, on this occasion, by his decision and courage. The reputation
Title:  earned by Washington in this battle was the principal cause of his being
Title:  selected to command the American armies at a later day. It is a circumstance
Title:  worthy of observation, that while all America rang with his well-merited
Title:  reputation, his name does not occur in any European account of the battle;
Title:  at least the author has searched for it without success. In this manner does
Title:  the mother country absorb even the fame, under that system of rule.
Title:  
Title:                 ----------------------------------------------
Title:  
Title:  Chapter 2
Title:  
Title:       Sola, sola, wo ha, ho, sola!
Title:  
Title:                                                             SHAKESPEARE
Title:  
Title:  While one of the lovely beings we have so cursorily presented to the reader
Title:  was thus lost in thought, the other quickly recovered from the alarm which
Title:  induced the exclamation, and, laughing at her own weakness, she inquired of
Title:  the youth who rode by her side:
Title:  
Title:  Are such specters frequent in the woods, Heyward, or is this sight an
Title:  especial entertainment ordered on our behalf? If the latter, gratitude must
Title:  close our mouths; but if the former, both Cora and I shall have need to draw
Title:  largely on that stock of hereditary courage which we boast, even before we
Title:  are made to encounter the redoubtable Montcalm.
Title:  
Title:  Yon Indian is a runner of the army; and, after the fashion of his people, he
Title:  may be accounted a hero, returned the officer. He has volunteered to guide
Title:  us to the lake, by a path but little known, sooner than if we followed the
Title:  tardy movements of the column; and, by consequence, more agreeably.
Title:  
Title:  I like him not, said the lady, shuddering, partly in assumed, yet more in
Title:  real terror. You know him, Duncan, or you would not trust yourself so freely
Title:  to his keeping?
Title:  
Title:  Say, rather, Alice, that I would not trust you. I do know him, or he would
Title:  not have my confidence, and least of all at this moment. He is said to be a
Title:  Canadian too; and yet he served with our friends the Mohawks, who, as you
Title:  know, are one of the six allied nations.1 He was brought among us, as I have
Title:  heard, by some strange accident in which your father was interested, and in
Title:  which the savage was rigidly dealt by; but I forget the idle tale, it is
Title:  enough, that he is now our friend.
Title:  
Title:  If he has been my father's enemy, I like him still less! exclaimed the now
Title:  really anxious girl. Will you not speak to him, Major Heyward, that I may
Title:  hear his tones? Foolish though it may be, you have often heard me avow my
Title:  faith in the tones of the human voice!
Title:  
Title:  It would be in vain; and answered, most probably, by an ejaculation. Though
Title:  he may understand it, he affects, like most of his people, to be ignorant of
Title:  the English; and least of all will he condescend to speak it, now that the
Title:  war demands the utmost exercise of his dignity. But he stops; the private
Title:  path by which we are to journey is, doubtless, at hand.
Title:  
Title:  The conjecture of Major Heyward was true. When they reached the spot where
Title:  the Indian stood, pointing into the thicket that fringed the military road;
Title:  a narrow and blind path, which might, with some little inconvenience,
Title:  receive one person at a time, became visible.
Title:  
Title:  Here, then, lies our way, said the young man, in a low voice. Manifest no
Title:  distrust, or you may invite the danger you appear to apprehend.
Title:  
Title:  Cora, what think you? asked the reluctant fair one. If we journey with the
Title:  troops, though we may find their presence irksome, shall we not feel better
Title:  assurance of our safety?
Title:  
Title:  Being little accustomed to the practices of the savages, Alice, you mistake
Title:  the place of real danger, said Heyward. If enemies have reached the portage
Title:  at all, a thing by no means probable, as our scouts are abroad, they will
Title:  surely be found skirting the column, where scalps abound the most. The route
Title:  of the detachment is known, while ours, having been determined within the
Title:  hour, must still be secret.
Title:  
Title:  Should we distrust the man because his manners are not our manners, and that
Title:  his skin is dark? coldly asked Cora.
Title:  
Title:  Alice hesitated no longer; but giving her Narrangansett2 a smart cut of the
Title:  whip, she was the first to dash aside the slight branches of the bushes, and
Title:  to follow the runner along the dark and tangled pathway. The young man
Title:  regarded the last speaker in open admiration, and even permitted her fairer,
Title:  though certainly not more beautiful companion, to proceed unattended, while
Title:  he sedulously opened the way himself for the passage of her who has been
Title:  called Cora. It would seem that the domestics had been previously
Title:  instructed; for, instead of penetrating the thicket, they followed the route
Title:  of the column; a measure which Heyward stated had been dictated by the
Title:  sagacity of their guide, in order to diminish the marks of their trail, if,
Title:  haply, the Canadian savages should be lurking so far in advance of their
Title:  army. For many minutes the intricacy of the route admitted of no further
Title:  dialogue; after which they emerged from the broad border of underbrush which
Title:  grew along the line of the highway, and entered under the high but dark
Title:  arches of the forest. Here their progress was less interrupted; and the
Title:  instant the guide perceived that the females could command their steeds, he
Title:  moved on, at a pace between a trot and a walk, and at a rate which kept the
Title:  sure-footed and peculiar animals they rode at a fast yet easy amble. The
Title:  youth had turned to speak to the dark-eyed Cora, when the distant sound of
Title:  horses; hoofs, clattering over the roots of the broken way in his rear,
Title:  caused him to check his charger; and, as his companions drew their reins at
Title:  the same instant, the whole party came to a halt, in order to obtain an
Title:  explanation of the unlooked-for interruption. In a few moments a colt was
Title:  seen gliding, like a fallow deer, among the straight trunks of the pines;
Title:  and, in another instant, the person of the ungainly man, described in the
Title:  preceding chapter, came into view, with as much rapidity as he could excite
Title:  his meager beast to endure without coming to an open rupture. Until now this
Title:  personage had escaped the observation of the travelers. If he possessed the
Title:  power to arrest any wandering eye when exhibiting the glories of his
Title:  altitude on foot, his equestrian graces were still more likely to attract
Title:  attention.
Title:  
Title:  Notwithstanding a constant application of his one armed heel to the flanks
Title:  of the mare, the most confirmed gait that he could establish was a
Title:  Canterbury gallop with the hind legs, in which those more forward assisted
Title:  for doubtful moments, though generally content to maintain a loping trot.
Title:  Perhaps the rapidity of the changes from one of these paces to the other
Title:  created an optical illusion, which might thus magnify the powers of the
Title:  beast; for it is certain that Heyward, who possessed a true eye for the
Title:  merits of a horse, was unable, with his utmost ingenuity, to decide by what
Title:  sort of movement his pursuer worked his sinuous way on his footsteps with
Title:  such persevering hardihood.
Title:  
Title:  The industry and movements of the rider were not less remarkable than those
Title:  of the ridden. At each change in the evolutions of the latter, the former
Title:  raised his tall person in the stirrups; producing, in this manner, by the
Title:  undue elongation of his legs, such sudden growths and diminishings of the
Title:  stature, as baffled every conjecture that might be made as to his
Title:  dimensions. If to this be added the fact that, in consequence of the ex
Title:  parte application of the spur, one side of the mare appeared to journey
Title:  faster than the other; and that the aggrieved flank was resolutely indicated
Title:  by unremitted flourishes of a bushy tail, we finish the picture of both
Title:  horse and man.
Title:  
Title:  The frown which had gathered around the handsome, open, and manly brow of
Title:  Heyward, gradually relaxed, and his lips curled into a slight smile, as he
Title:  regarded the stranger. Alice made no very powerful effort to control her
Title:  merriment; and even the dark, thoughtful eye of Cora lighted with a humor
Title:  that it would seem, the habit, rather than the nature, of its mistress
Title:  repressed.
Title:  
Title:  Seek you any here? demanded Heyward, when the other had arrived sufficiently
Title:  nigh to abate his speed; I trust you are no messenger of evil tidings?
Title:  
Title:  Even so, replied the stranger, making diligent use of his triangular castor,
Title:  to produce a circulation in the close air of the woods, and leaving his
Title:  hearers in doubt to which of the young man's questions he responded; when,
Title:  however, he had cooled his face, and recovered his breath, he continued, I
Title:  hear you are riding to William Henry; as I am journeying thitherward myself,
Title:  I concluded good company would seem consistent to the wishes of both
Title:  parties.
Title:  
Title:  You appear to possess the privilege of a casting vote, returned Heyward; we
Title:  are three, while you have consulted no one but yourself.
Title:  
Title:  Even so. The first point to be obtained is to know one's own mind. Once sure
Title:  of that, and where women are concerned it is not easy, the next is, to act
Title:  up to the decision. I have endeavored to do both, and here I am.
Title:  
Title:  If you journey to the lake, you have mistaken your route, said Heyward,
Title:  haughtily; the highway thither is at least half a mile behind you.
Title:  
Title:  Even so, returned the stranger, nothing daunted by this cold reception; I
Title:  have tarried at Edward a week, and I should be dumb not to have inquired the
Title:  road I was to journey; and if dumb there would be an end to my calling.
Title:  After simpering in a small way, like one whose modesty prohibited a more
Title:  open expression of his admiration of a witticism that was perfectly
Title:  unintelligible to his hearers, he continued, It is not prudent for any one
Title:  of my profession to be too familiar with those he has to instruct; for which
Title:  reason I follow not the line of the army; besides which, I conclude that a
Title:  gentleman of your character has the best judgment in matters of wayfaring; I
Title:  have, therefore, decided to join company, in order that the ride may be made
Title:  agreeable, and partake of social communion.
Title:  
Title:  A most arbitrary, if not a hasty decision! exclaimed Heyward, undecided
Title:  whether to give vent to his growing anger, or to laugh in the other's face.
Title:  But you speak of instruction, and of a profession; are you an adjunct to the
Title:  provincial corps, as a master of the noble science of defense and offense;
Title:  or, perhaps, you are one who draws lines and angles, under the pretense of
Title:  expounding the mathematics?
Title:  
Title:  The stranger regarded his interrogator a moment in wonder; and then, losing
Title:  every mark of self-satisfaction in an expression of solemn humility, he
Title:  answered:
Title:  
Title:  Of offense, I hope there is none, to either party: of defense, I make none -
Title:  by God's good mercy, having committed no palpable sin since last entreating
Title:  his pardoning grace. I understand not your allusions about lines and angles;
Title:  and I leave expounding to those who have been called and set apart for that
Title:  holy office. I lay claim to no higher gift than a small insight into the
Title:  glorious art of petitioning and thanksgiving, as practiced in psalmody.
Title:  
Title:  The man is, most manifestly, a disciple of Apollo, cried the amused Alice,
Title:  and I take him under my own especial protection. Nay, throw aside that
Title:  frown, Heyward, and in pity to my longing ears, suffer him to journey in our
Title:  train. Besides, she added, in a low and hurried voice, casting a glance at
Title:  the distant Cora, who slowly followed the footsteps of their silent, but
Title:  sullen guide, it may be a friend added to our strength, in time of need.
Title:  
Title:  Think you, Alice, that I would trust those I love by this secret path, did I
Title:  imagine such need could happen?
Title:  
Title:  Nay, nay, I think not of it now; but this strange man amuses me; and if he
Title:  hath music in his soul, let us not churlishly reject his company. She
Title:  pointed persuasively along the path with her riding whip, while their eyes
Title:  met in a look which the young man lingered a moment to prolong; then,
Title:  yielding to her gentle influence, he clapped his spurs into his charger, and
Title:  in a few bounds was again at the side of Cora.
Title:  
Title:  I am glad to encounter thee, friend, continued the maiden, waving her hand
Title:  to the stranger to proceed, as she urged her Narragansett to renew its
Title:  amble. Partial relatives have almost persuaded me that I am not entirely
Title:  worthless in a duet myself; and we may enliven our wayfaring by indulging in
Title:  our favorite pursuit. It might be of signal advantage to one, ignorant as I,
Title:  to hear the opinions and experience of a master in the art.
Title:  
Title:  It is refreshing both to the spirits and to the body to indulge in psalmody,
Title:  in befitting seasons, returned the master of song, unhesitatingly complying
Title:  with her intimation to follow; and nothing would relieve the mind more than
Title:  such a consoling communion. But four parts are altogether necessary to the
Title:  perfection of melody. You have all the manifestations of a soft and rich
Title:  treble; I can, by especial aid, carry a full tenor to the highest letter;
Title:  but we lack counter and bass! Yon officer of the king, who hesitated to
Title:  admit me to his company, might fill the latter, if one may judge from the
Title:  intonations of his voice in common dialogue.
Title:  
Title:  Judge not too rashly from hasty and deceptive appearances, said the lady,
Title:  smiling; though Major Heyward can assume such deep notes on occasion,
Title:  believe me, his natural tones are better fitted for a mellow tenor than the
Title:  bass you heard.
Title:  
Title:  Is he, then, much practiced in the art of psalmody? demanded her simple
Title:  companion.
Title:  
Title:  Alice felt disposed to laugh, though she succeeded in suppressing her
Title:  merriment, ere she answered:
Title:  
Title:  I apprehend that he is rather addicted to profane song. The chances of a
Title:  soldier's life are but little fitted for the encouragement of more sober
Title:  inclinations.
Title:  
Title:  Man's voice is given to him, like his other talents, to be used, and not to
Title:  be abused. None can say they have ever known me to neglect my gifts! I am
Title:  thankful that, though my boyhood may be said to have been set apart, like
Title:  the youth of the royal David, for the purposes of music, no syllable of rude
Title:  verse has ever profaned my lips.
Title:  
Title:  You have, then, limited your efforts to sacred song?
Title:  
Title:  Even so. As the psalms of David exceed all other language, so does the
Title:  psalmody that has been fitted to them by the divines and sages of the land,
Title:  surpass all vain poetry. Happily, I may say that I utter nothing but the
Title:  thoughts and the wishes of the King of Israel himself; for though the times
Title:  may call for some slight changes, yet does this version which we use in the
Title:  colonies of New England so much exceed all other versions, that, by its
Title:  richness, its exactness, and its spiritual simplicity, it approacheth, as
Title:  near as may be, to the great work of the inspired writer. I never abid in
Title:  any place, sleeping or waking, without an example of this gifted work. 'Tis
Title:  the six-and-twentieth edition, promulgated at Boston, Anno Domini 1744; and
Title:  is entitled, The Psalms, Hymns, and Spiritual Songs of the Old and New
Title:  Testaments; faithfully translated into English Metre, for the Use,
Title:  Edification, and Comfort of the Saints, in Public and Private, especially in
Title:  New England.
Title:  
Title:  During this eulogium on the rare production of his native poets, the
Title:  stranger had drawn the book from his pocket, and fitting a pair of
Title:  iron-rimmed spectacles to his nose, opened the volume with a care and
Title:  veneration suited to its sacred purposes. Then, without circumlocution or
Title:  apology, first pronounced the word Standish, and placing the unknown engine,
Title:  already described, to his mouth, from which he drew a high, shrill sound,
Title:  that was followed by an octave below, from his own voice, he commenced
Title:  singing the following words, in full, sweet, and melodious tones, that set
Title:  the music, the poetry, and even the uneasy motion of his ill-trained beast
Title:  at defiance;
Title:  
Title:       How good it is, O see,
Title:       And how it pleaseth well,
Title:       Together e'en in unity,
Title:       For brethren so to dwell.
Title:       It's like the choice ointment,
Title:       From the head to the beard did go;
Title:       Down Aaron's head, that downward went
Title:       His garment's skirts unto.
Title:  
Title:  The delivery of these skillful rhymes was accompanied, on the part of the
Title:  stranger, by a regular rise and fall of his right hand, which terminated at
Title:  the descent, by suffering the fingers to dwell a moment on the leaves of the
Title:  little volume; and on the ascent, by such a flourish of the member as none
Title:  but the initiated may ever hope to imitate. It would seem that long practice
Title:  had rendered this manual accompaniment necessary; for it did not cease until
Title:  the preposition which the poet had selected for the close of his verse had
Title:  been duly delivered like a word of two syllables.
Title:  
Title:  Such an innovation on the silence and retirement of the forest could not
Title:  fail to enlist the ears of those who jouneyed at so short a distance in
Title:  advance. The Indian muttered a few words in broken English to Heyward, who,
Title:  in his turn, spoke to the stranger; at once interrupting, and, for the time,
Title:  closing his musical efforts.
Title:  
Title:  Though we are not in danger, common prudence would teach us to journey
Title:  through this wilderness in as quiet a manner as possible. You will then,
Title:  pardon me, Alice, should I diminish your enjoyments, by requesting this
Title:  gentleman to postpone his chant until a safer opportunity.
Title:  
Title:  You will diminish them, indeed, returned the arch girl; for never did I hear
Title:  a more unworthy conjunction of execution and language than that to which I
Title:  have been listening; and I was far gone in a learned inquiry into the causes
Title:  of such an unfitness between sound and sense, when you broke the charm of my
Title:  musings by that bass of yours, Duncan!
Title:  
Title:  I know not what you call my bass, said Heyward, piqued at her remark, but I
Title:  know that your safety, and that of Cora, is far dearer to me than could be
Title:  any orchestra of Handel's music. He paused and turned his head quickly
Title:  toward a thicket, and then bent his eyes suspiciously on their guide, who
Title:  continued his steady pace, in undisturbed gravity. The young man smiled to
Title:  himself, for he belived he had mistaken some shining berry of the woods for
Title:  the glistening eyeballs of a prowling savage, and he rode forward,
Title:  continuing the conversation which had been interrupted by the passing
Title:  thought.
Title:  
Title:  Major Heyward was mistaken only in suffering his youthful and generous pride
Title:  to suppress his active watchfulness. The cavalcade had not long passed,
Title:  before the branches of the bushes that formed the thicket were cautiously
Title:  moved asunder, and a human visage, as fiercely wild as savage art and
Title:  unbridled passions could make it, peered out on the retiring footsteps of
Title:  the travelers. A gleam of exultation shot across the darkly-painted
Title:  lineaments of the inhabitant of the forest, as he traced the route of his
Title:  intended victims, who rode unconsciously onward, the light and graceful
Title:  forms of the females waving among the trees, in the curvatures of their
Title:  path, followed at each bend by the manly figure of Heyward, until, finally,
Title:  the shapeless person of the singing master was concealed behind the
Title:  numberless trunks of trees, that rose, in dark lines, in the intermediate
Title:  space.
Title:  
Title:  -----------------------
Title:  
Title:  1 There existed for a long time a confederation among the Indian tribes
Title:  which occupied the northwestern part of the colony of New York which was at
Title:  first known as the Five Nations. At a later day it admitted another tribe,
Title:  when the appellation was changed to that of the Six Nations. The original
Title:  confederation consisted of the Mohawks, the Oneidas, the Senecas, the
Title:  Cayugas, and the Onondagoes. The sixth tribe was the Tuscaroras. There are
Title:  remnants of all these people still living on lands secured to them by the
Title:  state; but they are daily disappearing, either by deaths or by removals to
Title:  scenes more congenial to their habits. In a short time there will be no
Title:  remains of these extraordinary people, in those regions in which they dwelt
Title:  for centuries, but their names. The state of New York has counties named
Title:  after all of them but the Mohawks and the Tuscaroras. The second river of
Title:  that state is called the Mohawk. 2 In the state of Rhode Island there is a
Title:  bay called Narragansett, so named after a powerful tribe of Indians, which
Title:  formerly dwelt on its banks. Accident, or one of those unaccountable freaks
Title:  which nature sometimes plays in the animal world, gave rise to a breed of
Title:  horses which were once well known in America, and distinguished by their
Title:  habit of pacing. Horses of this race were, and are still, in much request as
Title:  saddle horses, on account of their hardiness and the ease of their
Title:  movements. As they were also sure of foot, the Narragansetts were greatly
Title:  sought for by females who were obliged to travel over the roots and holes in
Title:  the new countries.
Title:  
Title:                 ----------------------------------------------
Title:  
Title:  Chapter 3
Title:  
Title:       Before these fields were shorn and till'd,
Title:       Full to the brim our rivers flow'd;
Title:       The melody of waters fill'd
Title:       The fresh and boundless wood;
Title:       And torrents dash'd, and rivulets play'd,
Title:       And fountains spouted in the shade.
Title:  
Title:                                                                  BRYANT
Title:  
Title:  Leaving the unsuspecting Heyward and his confiding companions to penetrate
Title:  still deeper into a forest that contained such treacherous inmates, we must
Title:  use an author's privilege, and shift the scene a few miles to the westward
Title:  of the place where we have last seen them.
Title:  
Title:  On that day, two men were lingering on the banks of a small but rapid
Title:  stream, within an hour's journey of the encampment of Webb, like those who
Title:  awaited the appearance of an absent person, or the approach of some expected
Title:  event. The vast canopy of woods spread itself to the margin of the river,
Title:  overhanging the water, and shadowing its dark current with a deeper hue. The
Title:  rays of the sun were beginning to grow less fierce, and the intense heat of
Title:  the day was lessened, as the cooler vapors of the springs and fountains rose
Title:  above their leafy beds, and rested in the atmosphere. Still that breathing
Title:  silence, which marks the drowsy sultriness of an American landscape in July,
Title:  pervaded the secluded spot, interrupted only by the low voices of the men,
Title:  the occasional and lazy tap of a woodpecker, the discordant cry of some
Title:  gaudy jay, or a swelling on the ear, from the dull roar of a distant
Title:  waterfall. These feeble and broken sounds were, however, too familiar to the
Title:  foresters to draw their attention from the more interesting matter of their
Title:  dialogue. While one of these loiterers showed the red skin and wild
Title:  accounterments of a native of the woods, the other exhibited, through the
Title:  mask of his rude and nearly savage equipments, the brighter, though
Title:  sun-burned and long-faced complexion of one who might claim descent from a
Title:  European parentage. The former was seated on the end of a mossy log, in a
Title:  posture that permitted him to heighten the effect of his earnest language,
Title:  by the calm but expressive gestures of an Indian engaged in debate. his
Title:  body, which was nearly naked, presented a terrific emblem of death, drawn in
Title:  intermingled colors of white and black. His closely-shaved head, on which no
Title:  other hair than the well-known and chivalrous scalping tuft1 was preserved,
Title:  was without ornament of any kind, with the exception of a solitary eagle's
Title:  plume, that crossed his crown, and depended over the left shoulder. A
Title:  tomahawk and scalping knife, of English manufacture, were in his girdle;
Title:  while a short military rifle, of that sort with which the policy of the
Title:  whites armed their savage allies, lay carelessly across his bare and sinewy
Title:  knee. The expanded chest, full formed limbs, and grave countenance of this
Title:  warrior, would denote that he had reached the vigor of his days, though no
Title:  symptoms of decay appeared to have yet weakened his manhood.
Title:  
Title:  The frame of the white man, judging by such parts as were not concealed by
Title:  his clothes, was like that of one who had known hardships and exertion from
Title:  his earliest youth. His person, though muscular, was rather attenuated than
Title:  full; but every nerve and muscle appeared strung and indurated by unremitted
Title:  exposure and toil. He wore a hunting shirt of forest-green, fringed with
Title:  faded yellow,2 and a summer cap of skins which had been shorn of their fur.
Title:  He also bore a knife in a girdle of wampum, like that which confined the
Title:  scanty garments of the Indian, but no tomahawk. His moccasins were
Title:  ornamented after the gay fashion of the natives, while the only part of his
Title:  under dress which appeared below the hunging frock was a pair of buckskin
Title:  leggings, that laced at the sides, and which were gartered above the knees,
Title:  with the sinews of a deer. A pouch and horn completed his personal
Title:  accouterments, though a rifle of great length,3 which the theory of the more
Title:  ingenious whites had taught them was the most dangerous of all firearms,
Title:  leaned against a neighboring sapling. The eye of the hunter, or scout,
Title:  whichever he might be, was small, quick, keen, and restless, roving while he
Title:  spoke, on every side of him, as if in quest of game, or distrusting the
Title:  sudden approach of some lurking enemy. Notwithstanding the symptoms of
Title:  habitual suspicion, his countenance was not only without guile, but at the
Title:  moment at which he is introduced, it was charged with an expression of
Title:  sturdy honesty. Even your traditions make the case in my favor,
Title:  Chingachgook, he said, speaking in the tongue which was known to all the
Title:  natives who formerly inhabited the country between the Hudson and the
Title:  Potomac, and of which we shall give a free translation for the benefit of
Title:  the reader; endeavoring, at the same time, to preserve some of the
Title:  peculiarities, both of the individual and of the language. Your fathers came
Title:  from the setting sun, crossed the big river,4 fought the people of the
Title:  country, and took the land; and mine came from the red sky of the morning,
Title:  over the salt lake, and did their work much after the fashion that had been
Title:  set them by yours; then let God judge the matter between us, and friends
Title:  spare their words! My fathers fought with the naked red man! returned the
Title:  Indian, sternly, in the same language. Is there no difference, Hawkeye,
Title:  between the stone-headed arrow of the warrior, and the leaden bullet with
Title:  which you kill?
Title:  
Title:  There is reason in an Indian, though nature has made him with a red skin!
Title:  said the white man, shaking his head like one on whom such an appeal to his
Title:  justice was not thrown away. For a moment he appeared to be conscious of
Title:  having the worst of the argument, then, rallying again, he answered the
Title:  objection of his antagonist in the best manner his limited information would
Title:  allow:
Title:  
Title:  I am no scholar, and I care not who knows it; but, judging from what I have
Title:  seen, at deer chases and squirrel hunts, of the sparks below, I should think
Title:  a rifle in the hands of their grandfathers was not so dangerous as a hickory
Title:  bow and a good flint-head might be, if drawn with Indian judgment, and sent
Title:  by an Indian eye.
Title:  
Title:  You have the story told by your fathers, returned the other, coldly waving
Title:  his hand. What say your old men? Do they tell the young warriors that the
Title:  pale faces met the red men, painted for war and armed with the stone hatchet
Title:  and wooden gun?
Title:  
Title:  I am not a prejudiced man, nor one who vaunts himself on his natural
Title:  privileges, though the worst enemy I have on earth, and he is an Iroquois,
Title:  daren't deny that I am genuine white, the scout replied, surveying, with
Title:  secret satisfaction, the faded color of his bony and sinewy hand, and I am
Title:  willing to own that my people have many ways, of which, as an honest man, I
Title:  can't approve. It is one of their customs to write in books what they have
Title:  done and seen, instead of telling them in their villages, where the lie can
Title:  be given to the face of a cowardly boaster, and the brave soldier can call
Title:  on his comrades to witness for the truth of his words. In consequence of
Title:  this bad fashion, a man, who is too conscientious to misspend his days among
Title:  the women, in learning the names of black marks, may never hear of the deeds
Title:  of his fathers, nor feel a pride in striving to outdo them. For myself, I
Title:  conclude the Bumppos could shoot, for I have a natural turn with a rifle,
Title:  which must have been handed down from generation to generation, as, our holy
Title:  commandments tell us, all good and evil gifts are bestowed; though I should
Title:  be loath to answer for other people in such a matter. But every story has
Title:  its two sides; so I ask you, Chingachgook, what passed, according to the
Title:  traditions of the red men, when our fathers first met?
Title:  
Title:  A silence of a minute succeeded, during which the Indian sat mute; then,
Title:  full of the dignity of his office, he commenced his brief tale, with a
Title:  solemnity that served to heighten its appearance of truth.
Title:  
Title:  Listen, Hawkeye, and your ear shall drink no lie. 'Tis what my fathers have
Title:  said, and what the Mohicans have done. He hesitated a single instant, and
Title:  bending a cautious glance toward his companion, he continued, in a manner
Title:  that was divided between interrogation and assertion. Does not this stream
Title:  at our feet run toward the summer, until its waters grow salt, and the
Title:  current flows upward?
Title:  
Title:  It can't be denied that your traditions tell you true in both these matters,
Title:  said the white man; for I have been there, and have seen them, though why
Title:  water, which is so sweet in the shade, should become bitter in the sun, is
Title:  an alteration for which I have never been able to account.
Title:  
Title:  And the current! demanded the Indian, who expected his reply with that sort
Title:  of interest that a man feels in the confirmation of testimony, at which he
Title:  marvels even while he respects it; the fathers of Chingachgook have not
Title:  lied!
Title:  
Title:  The holy Bible is not more true, and that is the truest thing in nature.
Title:  They call this up-stream current the tide, which is a thing soon explained,
Title:  and clear enough. Six hours the waters run in, and six hours they run out,
Title:  and the reason is this: when there is higher water in the sea than in the
Title:  river, they run in until the river gets to be highest, and then it runs out
Title:  again.
Title:  
Title:  The waters in the woods, and on the great lakes, run downward until they lie
Title:  like my hand, said the Indian, stretching the limb horizontally before him,
Title:  and then they run no more.
Title:  
Title:  No honest man will deny it, said the scout, a little nettled at the implied
Title:  distrust of his explanation of the mystery of the tides; and I grant that it
Title:  is true on the small scale, and where the land is level. But everything
Title:  depends on what scale you look at things. Now, on the small scale, the 'arth
Title:  is level; but on the large scale it is round. In this manner, pools and
Title:  ponds, and even the great fresh-water lakes, may be stagnant, as you and I
Title:  both know they are, having seen them; but when you come to spread water over
Title:  a great tract, like the sea, where the earth is round, how in reason can the
Title:  water be quiet? You might as well expect the river to lie still on the brink
Title:  of those black rocks a mile above us, though your own ears tell you that it
Title:  is tumbling over them at this very moment.
Title:  
Title:  If unsatisfied by the philosophy of his companion, the Indian was far too
Title:  dignified to betray his unbelief. He listened like one who was convinced,
Title:  and resumed his narrative in his former solemn manner.
Title:  
Title:  We came from the place where the sun is hid at night, over great plains
Title:  where the buffaloes live, until we reached the big river. There we fought
Title:  the Alligewi, till the ground was red with their blood. From the banks of
Title:  the big river to the shores of the salt lake, there was none to meet us. The
Title:  Maquas followed at a distance. We said the country should be ours from the
Title:  place where the water runs up no longer on this stream, to a river twenty
Title:  sun's journey toward the summer. We drove the Maquas into the woods with the
Title:  bears. They only tasted salt at the licks; they drew no fish from the great
Title:  lake; we threw them the bones.
Title:  
Title:  All this I have heard and believe, said the white man, observing that the
Title:  Indian paused; but it was long before the English came into the country.
Title:  
Title:  A pine grew then where this chestnut now stands. The first pale faces who
Title:  came among us spoke no English. They came in a large canoe, when my fathers
Title:  had buried the tomahawk with the red men around them. Then, Hawkeye, he
Title:  continued, betraying his deep emotion, only by permitting his voice to fall
Title:  to those low, guttural tones, which render his language, as spoken at times,
Title:  so very musical; then, Hawkeye, we were one people, and we were happy. The
Title:  salt lake gave us its fish, the wood its deer, and the air its birds. We
Title:  took wives who bore us children; we worshipped the Great Spirit; and we kept
Title:  the Maquas beyond the sound of our songs of triumph.
Title:  
Title:  Know you anything of your own family at that time? demanded the white. But
Title:  you are just a man, for an Indian; and as I suppose you hold their gifts,
Title:  your fathers must have been brave warriors, and wise men at the
Title:  council-fire.
Title:  
Title:  My tribe is the grandfather of nations, but I am an unmixed man. The blood
Title:  of chiefs is in my veins, where it must stay forever. The Dutch landed, and
Title:  gave my people the fire-water; they drank until the heavens and the earth
Title:  seemed to meet, and they foolishly thought they had found the Great Spirit.
Title:  Then they parted with their land. Foot by foot, they were driven back from
Title:  the shores, until I, that am a chief and a Sagamore, have never seen the sun
Title:  shine but through the trees, and have never visited the graves of my
Title:  fathers.
Title:  
Title:  Graves bring solemn feelings over the mind, returned the scout, a good deal
Title:  touched at the calm suffering of his companion; and they often aid a man in
Title:  his good intentions; though, for myself, I expect to leave my own bones
Title:  unburied, to bleach in the woods, or to be torn asunder by the wolves. But
Title:  where are to be found those of your race who came to their kin in the
Title:  Delaware country, so many summers since?
Title:  
Title:  Where are the blossoms of those summers! - fallen, one by one; so all of my
Title:  family departed, each in his turn, to the land of spirits. I am on the
Title:  hilltop and must go down into the valley; and when Uncas follows in my
Title:  footsteps there will no longer be any of the blood of the Sagamores, for my
Title:  boy is the last of the Mohicans.
Title:  
Title:  Uncas is here, said another voice, in the same soft, guttural tones, near
Title:  his elbow; who speaks to Uncas?
Title:  
Title:  The white man loosened his knife in his leathern sheath, and made an
Title:  involuntary movement of the hand toward his rifle, at this sudden
Title:  interruption; but the Indian sat composed, and without turning his head at
Title:  the unexpected sounds.
Title:  
Title:  At the next instant, a youthful warrior passed between them, with a
Title:  noiseless step, and seated himself on the bank of the rapid stream. No
Title:  exclamation of surprise escaped the father, nor was any question asked, or
Title:  reply given, for several minutes; each appearing to await the moment when he
Title:  might speak, without betraying womanish curiosity or childish impatience.
Title:  The white man seemed to take counsel from their customs, and, relinquishing
Title:  his grasp of the rifle, he also remained silent and reserved. At length
Title:  Chingachgook turned his eyes slowly toward his son, and demanded:
Title:  
Title:  Do the Maquas dare to leave the print of their moccasins in these woods?
Title:  
Title:  I have been on their trail, replied the young Indian, and know that they
Title:  number as many as the fingers of my two hands; but they lie hid like
Title:  cowards.
Title:  
Title:  The thieves are outlying for scalps and plunder, said the white man, whom we
Title:  shall call Hawkeye, after the manner of his companions. That busy Frenchman,
Title:  Montcalm, will send his spies into our very camp, but he will know what road
Title:  we travel!
Title:  
Title:  'Tis enough, returned the father, glancing his eye toward the setting sun;
Title:  they shall be driven like deer from their bushes. Hawkeye, let us eat
Title:  to-night, and show the Maquas that we are men to-morrow.
Title:  
Title:  I am as ready to do the one as the other; but to fight the Iroquois 'tis
Title:  necessary to find the skulkers; and to eat, 'tis necessary to get the game -
Title:  talk of the devil and he will come; there is a pair of the biggest antlers I
Title:  have seen this season, moving the bushes below the hill! Now, Uncas, he
Title:  continued, in a half whisper, and laughing with a kind of inward sound, like
Title:  one who had learned to be watchful, I will bet my charger three times full
Title:  of powder, against a foot of wampum, that I take him atwixt the eyes, and
Title:  nearer to the right than to the left.
Title:  
Title:  It cannot be! said the young Indian, springing to his feet with youthful
Title:  eagerness; all but the tips of his horns are hid!
Title:  
Title:  He's a boy! said the white man, shaking his head while he spoke, and
Title:  addressing the father. Does he think when a hunter sees a part of the
Title:  creatur, he can't tell where the rest of him should be!
Title:  
Title:  Adjusting his rifle, he was about to make an exhibition of that skill on
Title:  which he so much valued himself, when the warrior struck up the piece with
Title:  his hand, saying:
Title:  
Title:  Hawkeye! will you fight the Maquas?
Title:  
Title:  These Indians know the nature of the woods, as it might be by instinct!
Title:  returned the scout, dropping his rifle, and turning away like a man who was
Title:  convinced of his error. I must leave the buck to your arrow, Uncas, or we
Title:  may kill a deer for them thieves, the Iroquois, to eat.
Title:  
Title:  The instant the father seconded this intimation by an expressive gesture of
Title:  the hand, Uncas threw himself on the ground, and approached the animal with
Title:  wary movements. When within a few yards of the cover, he fitted an arrow to
Title:  his bow with the utmost care, while the antlers moved, as if their owner
Title:  snuffed an enemy in the tainted air. In another moment the twang of the cord
Title:  was heard, a white streak was seen glancing into the bushes, and the wounded
Title:  buck plunged from the cover, to the very feet of his hidden enemy. Avoiding
Title:  the horns of the infuriated animal, Uncas darted to his side, and passed his
Title:  knife across the throat, when bounding to the edge of the river it fell,
Title:  dyeing the waters with its blood.
Title:  
Title:  'Twas done with Indian skill, said the scout laughing inwardly, but with
Title:  vast satisfaction; and 'twas a pretty sight to behold! Though an arrow is a
Title:  near shot, and needs a knife to finish the work.
Title:  
Title:  Hugh! ejaculated his companion, turning quickly, like a hound who scented
Title:  game.
Title:  
Title:  By the Lord, there is a drove of them! exclaimed the scout, whose eyes began
Title:  to glisten with the ardor of his usual occupation; if they come within range
Title:  of a bullet I will drop one, though the whole Six Nations should be lurking
Title:  within sound! What do you hear, Chingachgook? for to my ears the woods are
Title:  dumb.
Title:  
Title:  There is but one deer, and he is dead, said the Indian, bending his body
Title:  till his ear nearly touched the earth. I hear the sounds of feet!
Title:  
Title:  Perhaps the wolves have driven the buck to shelter, and are following on his
Title:  trail.
Title:  
Title:  No. The horses of white men are coming! returned the other, raising himself
Title:  with dignity, and resuming his seat on the log with his former composure.
Title:  Hawkeye, they are your brothers; speak to them.
Title:  
Title:  That I will, and in English that the king needn't be ashamed to answer,
Title:  returned the hunter, speaking in the language of which he boasted; but I see
Title:  nothing, nor do I hear the sounds of man or beast; 'tis strange that an
Title:  Indian should understand white sounds better than a man who, his very
Title:  enemies will own, has no cross in his blood, although he may have lived with
Title:  the red skins long enough to be suspected! Ha! there goes something like the
Title:  cracking of a dry stick, too - now I hear the bushes move - yes, yes, there
Title:  is a trampling that I mistook for the falls - and - but here they come
Title:  themselves; God keep them from the Iroquois!
Title:  
Title:  -----------------------
Title:  
Title:  1 The North American warrior caused the hair to be plucked from his whole
Title:  body; a small tuft was left on the crown of his head, in order that his
Title:  enemy might avail himself of it, in wrenching off the scalp in the event of
